<div id="review_201762499" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201762499" />
    <a title="Malcolm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5860800-malcolm"><img alt="Malcolm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386870354p2/5860800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201762499?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Malcolm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Malcolm" href="/user/show/5860800-malcolm">Malcolm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780140254037" href="/book/show/858881.Pedagogy_of_the_Oppressed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860800-malcolm?shelf=marxism-and-the-left">marxism-and-the-left</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201762499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12117387857793791392">Writing a review of this is a challenge in part because the book has had such a powerful effect on my approach to education, and as such to everything I do as a University teacher. I come back to it regularly to remind myself that the topical fads in pedagogy (such as the claims made for student centred active learning, so hip in British HE in the late 2000s and early 2010s) are not as new as many of their protagonists claim. In many cases thay are a politically diluted version of a more deep se</span>
  <span id="freeText12117387857793791392" style="display:none">Writing a review of this is a challenge in part because the book has had such a powerful effect on my approach to education, and as such to everything I do as a University teacher. I come back to it regularly to remind myself that the topical fads in pedagogy (such as the claims made for student centred active learning, so hip in British HE in the late 2000s and early 2010s) are not as new as many of their protagonists claim. In many cases thay are a politically diluted version of a more deep seated radical pedagogy that without those radical politics lose their transformative potential and keep education as a training ground for capitalism's leaders and drones. Friere's work grew from his experiences working and teaching among the poor and oppressed of Brazil's favelas and countryside while his pedagogy and methodology have become increasingly important in a world where we are becoming increasingly alienated, overmanaged and dependent. I can say nothing more than read it, adapt it to your local conditions, and change them!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12117387857793791392" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3265396943" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3265396943" />
    <a title="Sasha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31533793-sasha"><img alt="Sasha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528070843p2/31533793.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3265396943?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sasha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sasha" href="/user/show/31533793-sasha">Sasha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780826406118" href="/book/show/77079.Pedagogy_of_the_Oppressed">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3265396943" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17863529284267463894">For me, the sum of this book's parts is greater than the whole. It is written from Freire's perspective as someone who taught adults in Brazil to read and write and is super influenced by Fanon and Marx. There is so much to be extrapolated from this text to all struggles for liberation, including our current focus on abolishing policing and prisons (which, unsurprisingly, was on my mind while reading this). Some highlights: <br/><br/>* "The concrete situation which begets oppression must be transformed." </span>
  <span id="freeText17863529284267463894" style="display:none">For me, the sum of this book's parts is greater than the whole. It is written from Freire's perspective as someone who taught adults in Brazil to read and write and is super influenced by Fanon and Marx. There is so much to be extrapolated from this text to all struggles for liberation, including our current focus on abolishing policing and prisons (which, unsurprisingly, was on my mind while reading this). Some highlights: <br /><br />* "The concrete situation which begets oppression must be transformed." <br />* "Acts which prevent the restoration of the oppressive regime cannot be compared with those which create and maintain it." <br />* The dominant elites "can condemn the violence of the strike and call upon the state in the same breath to use violence in putting down the strike." <br />* "The means used are not important; to alienate human beings from their own decision-making is to change them into objects." <br />* "What distinguishes revolutionary leaders from the dominant elite is not only their objectives but their procedures." <br /><br />And of course a ton of great stuff on the banking (teacher holds all the knowledge and deposits it into the students' brains) vs. problem-posing (teacher and student both teach each other and learn together, teacher-student poses questions which help the student-teachers question what they viewed as a pre-determined, static reality) approaches to education. <br /><br />Not everything holds up for me, but overall this rocked my world and was so resonant to read at this time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17863529284267463894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15725190227710972973">thank u for ur service mr freire .................</span>

<div id="review_1048590381" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1048590381" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14941179-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353981100p2/14941179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1048590381?book_show_action=true">Sep 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/14941179-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780140254037" href="/book/show/858881.Pedagogy_of_the_Oppressed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=teaching">teaching</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=theory">theory</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1048590381" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5937915648546311840">'Pedagogy of the Oppressed' was first published nearly fifty years ago, yet read to me as a fresh, powerful, and relevant text, both on a personal and social level. On the personal front, it reminded me of how much I prefer small group teaching to lecturing. In the former case, I used to facilitate and guide critical discussions on a topic with three or four students who had written an essay about it. It was obvious in such discussions whether the students had read about the topic, whether they </span>
  <span id="freeText5937915648546311840" style="display:none">'Pedagogy of the Oppressed' was first published nearly fifty years ago, yet read to me as a fresh, powerful, and relevant text, both on a personal and social level. On the personal front, it reminded me of how much I prefer small group teaching to lecturing. In the former case, I used to facilitate and guide critical discussions on a topic with three or four students who had written an essay about it. It was obvious in such discussions whether the students had read about the topic, whether they were interested, and what they thought. In the latter case, I talk at twenty or more students for two hours without any sense of whether they're listening, let alone learning. Freire emphasises dialogue as central to education, which I strongly concur with. I learn best from reading or talking, not listening. Likewise, I teach best through discussion, not lectures. It's a real pity that in the UK only Oxford and Cambridge give undergraduate students the opportunity to learn through such small group teaching. Moreover, I greatly appreciated Freire's points about 'problem-posing' education, which doesn't treat social issues as resolved. I used to genuinely enjoy discussing the flaws in housing policy, for example, with students. In short, I found Freire's angle on education inspiring, which is helpful as I'd been feeling quite jaded about academic teaching.<br /><br />More importantly, though, Freire's theories around oppression and resistance have a lot of resonance in 2017. Generally, I appreciated his rejection of binary divisions (for example objective and subjective) in favour of dialectics and the constant emphasis on dialogue. This is expressed with great elegance, for instance in chapter 3:<br /><br /><blockquote>Someone who cannot acknowledge himself to be mortal as everyone else still has a long way to go before he can reach the point of encounter. At the point of encounter there are neither utter ignoramuses nor perfect sages; there are only people who are attempting, together, to learn more than they now know. Dialogue further requires an intense faith in humankind, faith in their power to make and remake, to create and re-create, faith in their vocation to be more fully human (which is not the privilege of an elite, but the birthright of all). </blockquote><br /><br />Given the current quality of political and media debate, actual dialogue seems to be badly needed. Both in education and in culture more generally, I think there is a confusion between dialogue and debate. A debate is competitive and the aim is to win; dialogue is co-operative and all participants gain from it. The present forms taken by the media and the structures through which we interact online seem to greatly inhibit dialogue as Freire describes it; everything becomes a polarised and aggressively combative debate. I found his emphasis on dialogue inspiring, both as a teacher and a person, as well as nuanced. The role of critical thinking in dialogue was very well articulated:<br /><br /><blockquote>Finally, true dialogue cannot exist unless the dialoguers engage in critical thinking – thinking which discerns and indivisible solidarity between the world and the people and admits of no dichotomy between them – thinking which perceives reality as a process, as transformation, rather than as a static entity – thinking which does not separate itself from action, but constantly immerses itself in temporality without fear of the risks involved. Critical thinking contrasts with naive thinking, which sees 'historical time as a weight, a stratification of the acquisitions and experiences of the past', from which the present should emerge normalised and 'well-behaved'. For the naive thinker, the important thing is accommodation to this normalised 'today'. For the critic, the important thing is the continuing transformation of reality.</blockquote><br /><br />More worryingly, this analysis feels very apposite to the neo-fascist resurgence currently occurring in Europe and America:<br /><br /><blockquote>...the former oppressors do not feel liberated. On the contrary, they genuinely consider themselves to be oppressed. Conditioned by the experience of oppressing others, any situation other than their former seems to them like oppression. Formerly, they could eat, dress, wear shoes, be educated, travel, and hear Beethoven; while millions did not eat, had no clothes or shoes, neither studied nor travelled, much less listened to Beethoven. Any restriction on this way of life, in the name of the rights of the community, appears to the former oppressors as a profound violation of their individual rights – although they had no respect for the millions who suffered and died of hunger, pain, sorrow, and despair. For the oppressors, 'human beings' refers only to themselves; other people are 'things'.</blockquote><br /><br />This is a wise and thought-provoking book. I think it especially significant for those from developed countries who are working, perhaps for NGOs, in the developing world. No lasting improvements can be made without partnership and dialogue, rather than those with money dictating to those without.<br /><br />A further comment that rang very true at the present moment concerns making the state of the world mythical. This is especially true of neoliberal capitalism.<br /><br /><blockquote>In order to present for the consideration of the oppressed and subjugated a world of deceit designed to increase alienation and passivity, the oppressors develop a series of methods precluding any presentation of the world as a problem and showing it rather as a fixed entity, as something given – something to which people, as mere spectators, must adapt. […] It is accomplished by the oppressors' depositing myths indispensable to the preservation of the status quo: for example, the myth that the oppressive order is a 'free society'; the myth that all persons are free to work where they wish, that if they don't like their boss they can leave him and find another job; the myth that this order respects human rights and is therefore worthy of esteem; the myth that everyone who is industrious can become an entrepreneur – worse yet, the myth that the street vendor is as much an entrepreneur as the owner of a large factory...</blockquote><br /><br />These myths are still alive and well, thus 'Pedagogy of the Oppressed' remains inspiring and important. It caused me to reflect on the importance of education, formal and informal, for the changing of minds individually and collectively. I read most of it on a train while very tired, though, so feel that it deserves a re-read so I can think about it in greater depth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5937915648546311840" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_37517224" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/37517224" />
    <a title="Amy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/478604-amy"><img alt="Amy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1209523374p2/478604.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37517224?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy" href="/user/show/478604-amy">Amy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/478604-amy?shelf=edci-886">edci-886</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/478604-amy?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/478604-amy?shelf=education">education</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37517224" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13461890556881931192">This book has been on my reading list for the past year based on great recommendations on Goodreads; I’m happy to say that it lived up to the hype. I started to get hooked reading the introduction by Donald Macedo. His use of the term “culturally schizophrenic” resonates with me because it reminds me (a little bit) of how I felt as a woman, working in engineering, in the Navy. I felt compartmentalized, like the different parts of my world did not connect. I don’t think it was exactly the same fe</span>
  <span id="freeText13461890556881931192" style="display:none">This book has been on my reading list for the past year based on great recommendations on Goodreads; I’m happy to say that it lived up to the hype. I started to get hooked reading the introduction by Donald Macedo. His use of the term “culturally schizophrenic” resonates with me because it reminds me (a little bit) of how I felt as a woman, working in engineering, in the Navy. I felt compartmentalized, like the different parts of my world did not connect. I don’t think it was exactly the same feeling Macedo was describing, but it gave me a reference point for thinking about situations where two diverse cultures clash. <br />	Also in the introduction, Macedo discusses how students in some parts of the world risk punishment and imprisonment to read Pedagogy of the Oppressed, while in most United States undergraduate and graduate programs, it is omitted. My first instinct when reading this was very cynical; of course it was omitted, because educational leaders are scared. If people read Freire and understand how full of domination the banking model of education is, they’ll be hesitant to become part of the U.S. school system. Students will be overcome by despair, and the colleges of education will lose the next generation of school teachers. I was thinking of this from an education as social reproduction viewpoint.<br />	When I got to the preface, written by Freire, I was awed to find that he had a response for my cynicism. He talks about the fear of freedom – particularly, he talks about the views held by oppressors that “It is better for the victims of injustice not to recognize themselves as such (Freire, 2000, p. 36).” I could see that my response came from my own personal fears, combined with a lack of faith in the students pursuing careers in teaching. <br />	This book has given me a completely new way to look at educational practices. From assignments and discussions in my methods class to my daughter’s parent teacher conference, I have started to see ways in which education could be more liberatory and less an exercise of power and domination. I don’t mean to imply that my methods teacher or my daughter’s teacher are unusually domineering – mostly, I’m thinking of things that are systemic and related to a banking system of education or are antidialogical in nature. <br />	The language at times was difficult; this is a dense book, packed with thoughts and ideas. I had to read it slowly and will be reading it again in the future. <br />	Personally, this book also provided a good answer to a question I’ve been asking teachers: why don’t we have national standards in education? On the surface, with a highly mobile society and increasing demands on teachers’ time, I thought that national standards would keep every state and local school district from reinventing the wheel periodically, allowing the educational focus to be on children. Freire’s idea that the people have the responsibility to decide what they need to know (based on the process of identifying generative themes) made me see the potential for oppression and irrelevance in adoption of national standards. In one of those strange coincidences of life, I then found an opinion article on the New York Times website about a national standards project: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://roomfordebate.blogs.nytimes.com/2009/09/22/national-academic-standards-the-first-test/">http://roomfordebate.blogs.nytimes.co...</a>.<br />	I came away from reading this book feeling uplifted and excited about the possibilities of practicing freedom as a teacher; at the same time, I’m daunted by some of the systemic obstacles that I will face. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13461890556881931192" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_222802837" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/222802837" />
    <a title="Rei Avocado" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3682119-rei-avocado"><img alt="Rei Avocado" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493223328p2/3682119.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/222802837?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rei Avocado" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rei Avocado" href="/user/show/3682119-rei-avocado">Rei Avocado</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3682119-rei-avocado?shelf=marxist-theory-philosophy">marxist-theory-philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3682119-rei-avocado?shelf=education-pedagogy">education-pedagogy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer222802837" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15249571389754790254">everything i say about this book seems trite in comparison to its contents. paulo freire was brilliant. absolutely brilliant. his methods for investigation, research, and ultimately educating the oppressed are NOT blueprints that must be followed strictly, but guidelines to liberation. what ive learned reading pedagogy of the oppressed is that one must be humble above all, and that i will most certainly have to read this again.<br/><br/>now i get it. now i truly understand why brazil is trying to ban frei</span>
  <span id="freeText15249571389754790254" style="display:none">everything i say about this book seems trite in comparison to its contents. paulo freire was brilliant. absolutely brilliant. his methods for investigation, research, and ultimately educating the oppressed are NOT blueprints that must be followed strictly, but guidelines to liberation. what ive learned reading pedagogy of the oppressed is that one must be humble above all, and that i will most certainly have to read this again.<br /><br />now i get it. now i truly understand why brazil is trying to ban freire from classrooms once again. i understand it. this book is so incredibly dangerous to the ruling classes that they have to ban it from history. that alone should be enough of a reason to read it immediately. as a brazilian teacher living during these years of lead, i can only hope that we can survive these dark times together, and freires writings will remain my guiding light.<br /><br />an addendum: i listened to the 50th anniversary edition audiobook on scribd and i highly recommend it. not only does it provide tons of great context for freires life and thought process and interviews with other educators around the world, it is by far the best translation i have seen for this book. portuguese to english translations are generally extremely poor for whatever reason (i remember giving up on an english edition of saramagos blindness in the first chapter) and when i attempted to read an earlier translation of pedagogy of the oppressed, i had a tremendous amount of difficulty with it. i can tell what are brazilianisms sloppily translated into english, because that is my day job (i teach ESL). and its disappointing that it took me so long to find a proper translation of one of the most important books ever written.<br /><br />the reason ive looked so hard for a good translation is because i want to be able to recommend an edition to organizer friends abroad rather than obliging them to learn portuguese to get the most out of an incredible concept, or have me filter information through them. (my next read will be the portuguese edition and yes i have to reread this book.)<br /><br />that being said, i have to touch on freires insistence and refusal to translate key concepts like conscientização. if you are the sort of person who whines about how communist jargon is sooo difficult then i ask you to give up on being any sort of leftist at all. his refusal to translate a portuguese word is specifically to force gringos out of complacency, to recognize that they are both oppressor and oppressed, and it is such a small gesture yet it has massive repercussions in how freires work should be approached.<br /><br />this book is of fundamental importance especially now. approach with an open mind and an open heart. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15249571389754790254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_71419161" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/71419161" />
    <a title="Ioana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1721356-ioana"><img alt="Ioana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505655996p2/1721356.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/71419161?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ioana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ioana" href="/user/show/1721356-ioana">Ioana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=curriculum">curriculum</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=education-policy">education-policy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=dissertation-general">dissertation-general</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1721356-ioana?shelf=post-colonialism">post-colonialism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer71419161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10169655407366827390">I've read and reread this book many times over since being an ed policy grad school (this period of my life started about six years ago). Unquestionably Freire, and specifically Pedagogy of the Oppressed, is one of THE most foundational writers/works in the tradition of critical pedagogy; in many ways it's a book that started the application of critical studies (Adorno, Horkheimer, Habermas) to education/ed policy, so on that account it is a must read, and also on that account, I give it a five </span>
  <span id="freeText10169655407366827390" style="display:none">I've read and reread this book many times over since being an ed policy grad school (this period of my life started about six years ago). Unquestionably Freire, and specifically Pedagogy of the Oppressed, is one of THE most foundational writers/works in the tradition of critical pedagogy; in many ways it's a book that started the application of critical studies (Adorno, Horkheimer, Habermas) to education/ed policy, so on that account it is a must read, and also on that account, I give it a five stars. <br /><br />Freire has been dissected and critiqued in too many ways to count since this book was first published (1968 in Portuguese/1970 in English). Feminists have taken issue with his unquestioning stance towards patriarchal and sexist root metaphors; those interested in more holistic and place conscious education have taken issue with Freire's anthropocentricity (which is a major feature of objectivist/dualist "Western" worldviews); others have taken issue with Freire's focus on progress and liberation at the expense of conservatism (i.e. conserving constructive cultural traditions which are part of communities' identities; in other words some progressives argue that we need a balance between emancipation AND conserving that which gives our traditions meaning; critical doesn't mean liberation from the past, it means being critical about what traditions to keep and which to let go).<br /><br />I see great value in all these critiques and think they justly apply to Paulo Freire. However, as bell hooks wrote, just because Freire's work is riddled with problems only means we must engage with it critically (as Freire himself wrote), not that we should avoid it. in Teaching to Transgress, hooks writes "There is no need to apologize for [Freire's] sexism. Freire's own model of critical pedagogy invites a critical interrogation of this flaw in the work. But critical interrogation is not the same as dismissal.... I would never wish to see a critique of this blind spot overshadown anyone's capacity to learn from the insights.." (1994, p. 49).<br /><br />ANYONE interested in social justice, emancipatory education, and a vision for an alternative to our current standardized, commodified, objectifying education culture, anyone teaching in urban or low income areas should read this book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10169655407366827390" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('wCMkMUrVOZAe3kZnFDRpy956aZqHcotj1L7TqpF0Ju3kg+8ShOGJMoT+i5NrBdbPjsWJZEQSS2+VchZ/M+t2ZA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ukq9Q68zXhQ7geRKCzv8CB7XE7L5GFBSbMy5e33vWsh26nZgYQfutqGhKb50CkMMTmjzTDp4kF4tAHyu33AKQQ==')})
  }

Fanon\'s masterwork is a classic alongside Edward Said\'s Orientalism or The Autobiography of Malcolm X, and it is now available in a new translation that updates its language for a new generation of readers.\n\nThe Wretched of the Earth is a brilliant analysis of the psychology of the colonized and their path to liberation. Bearing singular insight into the rage and frustration of colonized peoples, and the role of violence in effecting historical change, the book incisively attacks the twin perils of post-independence colonial politics: the disenfranchisement of the masses by the elites on the one hand, and intertribal and interfaith animosities on the other.\n\nFanon\'s analysis, a veritable handbook of social reorganization for leaders of emerging nations, has been reflected all too clearly in the corruption and violence that has plagued present-day Africa. The Wretched of the Earth has had a major impact on civil rights, anticolonialism, and black consciousness movements around the world, and this bold new translation by Richard Philcox reaffirms it as Loosely based on the form of Chaucer\'s Canterbury Tales, Dawkins\'s Tale takes us modern humans back through four billion years of life on our planet. As the pilgrimage progresses, we join with other organisms at the forty &quot;rendezvous points&quot; where we find a common ancestor. The band of pilgrims swells into a vast crowd as we join first with other primates, then with other mammals, and so on back to the first primordial organism.\n\nDawkins\'s brilliant, inventive approach allows us to view the connections between ourselves and all other life in a bracingly novel way. It also lets him shed bright new light on the most compelling aspects of evolutionary history and theory: sexual selection, speciation, convergent evolution, extinction, genetics, plate tectonics, geographical dispersal, and more. The Ancestor\'s Tale is at once a far-reaching survey of the latest, best thinking on biology and a fascinating history of life on Earth. Davis has put the case for the latest abolition movement in American life: the abolition of the prison. As she quite correctly notes, American life is replete with abolition movements, and when they were engaged in these struggles, their chances of success seemed almost unthinkable. For generations of Americans, the abolition of slavery was sheerest illusion. Similarly,the entrenched system of racial segregation seemed to last forever, and generations lived in the midst of the practice, with few predicting its passage from custom. The brutal, exploitative (dare one say lucrative?) convict-lease system that succeeded formal slavery reaped millions to southern jurisdictions (and untold miseries for tens of thousands of men, and women). Few predicted its passing from the American penal landscape. Davis expertly argues how social movements transformed these social, political and cultural institutions, and made such practices untenable.In Are Prisons Obsolete?, Professor Davis seeks to illustrate that the time for the prison is approaching an end. She argues forthrightly for &quot;decarceration&quot;, and argues for the transformation of the society as a whole.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2844036007558622866\" href=\"#\" Our leaders have shown themselves willing-as in the Cuban missile crisis-to follow the dream of dominance no matter how high the risks. World-renowned intellectual Noam Chomsky investigates how we came to this perilous moment and why our rulers are willing to jeopardize the future of our species.\n\nWith the striking logic that is his trademark, Chomsky tracks the U.S. government\'s aggressive pursuit of &quot;full spectrum dominance&quot; and vividly lays out how the most recent manifestations of the politics of global control-from unilateralism to the dismantling of international agreements to state terrorism-cohere in a drive for hegemony that ultimately threatens our existence. Power is the ultimate determinant in human society, being basic to the relations within any group and between groups. It implies the ability to defend one\'s interests and if necessary to impose one’s will by any means available. In relations between peoples, the question of power determines maneuverability in bargaining, the extent to which a people survive as a physical and cultural entity. When one society finds itself forced to relinquish power entirely to another society, that in itself is a form of underdevelopment.\nBefore a bomb ended his life in the summer of 1980, Walter Rodney had created a powerful legacy. This pivotal work, How Europe Underdeveloped Africa, had already brought a new perspective to the question of underdevelopment in Africa. his Marxist analysis went far beyond the heretofore accepted approach in the study of Third World underdevelopment.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/41108.Paulo_Freire">About Paulo Freire</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/41108.Paulo_Freire"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1221544351p3/41108.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/41108.Paulo_Freire">Paulo Freire
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
837
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13643763664848565542">The Brazilian educator Paulo Freire is among most the influential educational thinkers of the late 20th century. Born in Recife, Brazil, on September 19, 1921, Freire died of heart failure in Sao Paulo, Brazil on May 2, 1997. After a brief career as a lawyer, he taught Portuguese in secondary schools from 1941-1947. He subsequently became active in adult education and workers' training, and became</span>
  <span id="freeText13643763664848565542" style="display:none">The Brazilian educator Paulo Freire is among most the influential educational thinkers of the late 20th century. Born in Recife, Brazil, on September 19, 1921, Freire died of heart failure in Sao Paulo, Brazil on May 2, 1997. After a brief career as a lawyer, he taught Portuguese in secondary schools from 1941-1947. He subsequently became active in adult education and workers' training, and became the first Director of the Department of Cultural Extension of the University of Recife (1961-1964).<br /><br />Freire quickly gained international recognition for his experiences in literacy training in Northeastern Brazil. Following the military coup d'etat of 1964, he was jailed by the new government and eventually forced into a political exile that lasted fifteen-years.<br /><br />In 1969 he was a visiting scholar at Harvard University and then moved to Geneva, Switzerland where he assumed the role of special educational adviser to the World Congress of Churches. He returned to Brazil in 1979.<br /><br />Freire's most well known work is Pedagogy of the Oppressed (1970). Throughout this and subsequent books, he argues for system of education that emphasizes learning as an act of culture and freedom. He is most well known for concepts such as "Banking" Education, in which passive learners have pre-selected knowledge deposited in their minds; "Conscientization", a process by which the learner advances towards critical consciousness; the "Culture of Silence", in which dominated individuals lose the means by which to critically respond to the culture that is forced on them by a dominant culture. Other important concepts developed by Freire include: "Dialectic", "Empowerment", "Generative Themes/Words", "Humanization", "Liberatory Education", "Mystification", "Praxis", " Problematization", and "Transformation of the World".<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.education.miami.edu/ep/contemporaryed/Paulo_Freire/paulo_freire.html">http://www.education.miami.edu/ep/con...</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13643763664848565542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
